> Project Context: Figma UX Challenge — Design for an Astronaut                 
Challenge Overview                                                              
This is a UI/UX design competition in partnership with the Figma Education      
Program. The goal is to design a mobile or tablet app that helps astronauts     
manage the psychological and behavioral effects of long-duration isolation and  
confinement in deep space.                                                      
The app must address at least one of these NASA-researched problem areas:       
                                                                                
Behavioral health monitoring (detecting early signs of psychological stress)    
Sleep and circadian support (managing sleep disruption and light therapy)       
Connection and communication (crew cohesion and Earth-contact despite           
communication delays)                                                           
Cognitive workload management (reducing fatigue during high-demand phases)      
Mindfulness and mental restoration (journaling, nature simulation, space        
gardening, etc.)                                                                
                                                                                
Submission Deliverables Required                                                
                                                                                
FigJam brainstorm (research and problem framing)                                
Core user flow: 3–5 connected screens covering the primary use case             
Design system starter: consistent visual language for space context (low-light  
legibility, glove-friendly touch targets, low-distraction interfaces)           
Final prototype using Figma Design, Figma Make, or partially/fully developed    
code                                                                            
Video walkthrough of all process steps + shareable Figma links                  
                                                                                
Key Design Inspiration: Meta's TRIBE v2                                         
The design decisions in this project are informed by Meta FAIR's TRIBE v2       
(Trimodal Brain Encoder v2), a recently released open-source neuroscience AI    
model. Key facts to reference:                                                  
                                                                                
TRIBE v2 predicts how the human brain responds to visual, audio, and language   
stimuli using fMRI data from 700+ volunteers                                    
It maps 5 core functional brain networks: primary auditory, language, motion,   
default mode, and visual                                                        
It offers 70x higher spatial resolution than previous models                    
It supports zero-shot generalization across new subjects, languages, and tasks  
Meta has open-sourced the model, weights, code, and a live demo                 
                                                                                
How TRIBE v2 informs the UX design:                                             
                                                                                
Justifies keeping motion minimal during high-cognitive-load screens (motion     
network gets taxed)                                                             
Supports use of multi-sensory stimuli (nature sounds + visuals) for mental      
restoration features                                                            
Backs the behavioral health monitoring use case — a model like TRIBE v2 is the  
kind of AI that could power stress-detection features in a real implementation  
Grounds the design system decisions in neuroscience (e.g., language-light       
iconography to reduce cognitive load)                                           
TRIBE v2 is referenced as research inspiration, not as something being          
implemented in the prototype                                                    
                                                                                
Tooling Setup                                                                   
                                                                                
Claude Code CLI is connected to Figma via the official Figma MCP server         
(remote)                                                                        
MCP was added with: claude mcp add --scope user --transport http figma          
https://mcp.figma.com/mcp                                                       
The Figma plugin for Claude Code was installed via: claude plugin install       
figma@claude-plugins-official                                                   
This enables two-way sync: Claude Code can read Figma frames and push generated 
 UI back to the Figma canvas as editable layers                                 
Write-to-canvas is currently free during Figma's beta period                    
                                                                                
What's needed from Claude Code                                                  
Help building the prototype and design assets for this astronaut mental health  
app, using the Figma MCP connection to read design context and generate/push UI 
 components. The design should reflect the neuroscience principles from TRIBE   
v2 and meet the submission requirements above. read the pdf as well

     Figma UX Challenge — Astronaut Mental Health App: "Solace"

     Context

     Aaron is entering the Figma Education Program UX Challenge to design a
     mobile/tablet app helping astronauts manage psychological effects of
     long-duration space isolation. The project requires 5 deliverables (FigJam
     brainstorm, 5-screen user flow, design system starter, final prototype,
     video walkthrough). Design decisions are grounded in Meta FAIR's TRIBE v2
     neuroscience model. Claude Code is connected to Figma via MCP for two-way
     design sync.

     Critical prerequisite: Aaron's current Figma account is Starter/View (6 MCP
      reads/month). He must activate the Figma Education plan at
     https://www.figma.com/education/ for full access before we begin creating
     assets.

     ---
     App Concept: "Solace"

     An astronaut mental wellness companion addressing 3 problem areas:
     - Primary: Mindfulness & Mental Restoration
     - Secondary: Behavioral Health Monitoring
     - Secondary: Sleep & Circadian Support

     TRIBE v2 Neuroscience Principles Applied

     ┌─────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
     │   Brain     │                    Design Implication                     │
     │   Network   │                                                           │
     ├─────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
     │ Motion      │ Minimal animation during cognitive-load screens; subtle   │
     │             │ transitions only                                          │
     ├─────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
     │ Language    │ Icon-forward navigation; reduce text density to lower     │
     │             │ language-processing load                                  │
     ├─────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
     │ Default     │ Restoration features engage the default mode network      │
     │ Mode        │ (nature scenes, journaling)                               │
     ├─────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
     │ Auditory    │ Multi-sensory restoration: nature soundscapes paired with │
     │             │  visuals                                                  │
     ├─────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
     │ Visual      │ High-contrast dark theme; calming color palette; avoid    │
     │             │ visual clutter                                            │
     └─────────────┴───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

     ---
     5-Screen Core User Flow

     1. Home Dashboard — Daily wellness snapshot: mood ring, sleep score, next
     activity, crew pulse
     2. Check-In — Behavioral health assessment: visual mood/stress/energy
     selectors (no heavy text)
     3. Restore — Mental restoration hub: nature soundscapes, guided breathing,
     journaling, Earth-view sim
     4. Sleep Center — Circadian rhythm tracker, light therapy schedule, sleep
     trends
     5. Crew Hub — Async crew messaging, shared gratitude wall, Earth-contact
     queue with delay indicator

     ---
     Design System (Space-Optimized)

     Colors (Dark Theme)

     - Background: Deep navy #0B1426 → Dark space #060D19
     - Surface: #111D33 (cards, panels)
     - Primary accent: Soft teal #4FD1C5 (calming, high-visibility in low light)
     - Secondary accent: Warm amber #F6AD55 (alerts, warmth, human connection)
     - Success: Soft green #68D391
     - Danger: Muted coral #FC8181
     - Text primary: #E2E8F0 (high contrast on dark)
     - Text secondary: #A0AEC0

     Typography

     - Headings: Inter Bold, 20-28px
     - Body: Inter Regular, 16-18px
     - Caption: Inter Medium, 12-14px
     - Line height: 1.5 for body (readability in low-light)

     Spacing & Layout

     - 4px base grid
     - Touch targets: minimum 48px (glove-friendly)
     - Card padding: 16-24px
     - Screen padding: 20px horizontal
     - Tab bar height: 64px (glove-friendly)

     Components Needed

     - StatusCard (mood, sleep, workload indicators)
     - ActionButton (primary, secondary, ghost variants)
     - TabBar (5 icon tabs, no labels — language-light per TRIBE v2)
     - MoodSelector (visual emoji/gradient picker)
     - BreathingAnimation (subtle, non-distracting)
     - SoundscapeCard (nature scene thumbnail + audio indicator)
     - MessageBubble (async crew messages with delay indicator)
     - CircadianGraph (24h rhythm visualization)

     ---
     Execution Plan

     Phase 0: Prerequisites

     - Aaron activated Figma Education plan (DONE)
     - Verify upgraded seat with whoami

     Confirmed Decisions

     - Focus areas: All three (Mindfulness/Restoration + Behavioral Health +
     Sleep/Circadian)
     - Prototype approach: Hybrid (coded HTML/Tailwind locally + push screens to
      Figma)
     - Device target: Tablet (primary)

     Phase 1: FigJam Brainstorm (Deliverable 1)

     - Create a FigJam file via create_new_file
     - Generate research/problem-framing diagram via generate_diagram:
       - Flowchart showing: NASA problem areas → TRIBE v2 insights → Design
     decisions → App features
       - User persona flow: Astronaut daily journey through the app
     - This covers the "research and problem framing brainstorm" requirement

     Phase 2: Design System Starter (Deliverable 3)

     - Create a Figma Design file via create_new_file
     - Use use_figma Plugin API to programmatically create:
       - Color variables (all palette tokens)
       - Typography styles
       - Spacing tokens
       - Core component library (buttons, cards, tab bar, selectors)
     - Load the figma-use skill before each use_figma call

     Phase 3: Core Screens — Coded Prototype (Deliverables 2 & 4)

     - Build a local HTML/CSS/JS prototype with all 5 screens
       - Use Tailwind CSS via CDN for rapid styling
       - Dark theme matching design system tokens
       - Simple client-side routing between screens
       - Tablet-first layout (1024x768 viewport)
       - Landscape orientation optimized for spacecraft mounting
     - Run local dev server
     - Capture each screen into Figma via generate_figma_design
     - This satisfies both "core user flow" and "partially developed prototype"

     Phase 4: Polish & Connect

     - Review captured screens in Figma
     - Add any refinements via use_figma
     - Ensure all screens are connected in a logical flow

     Phase 5: Video Prep (User's responsibility)

     - Provide Aaron with a walkthrough script covering:
       a. Problem framing (FigJam board)
       b. Design system decisions (Figma Design)
       c. Prototype demo (code + Figma screens)
       d. TRIBE v2 neuroscience justifications

     ---
     Verification

     - FigJam brainstorm file exists with research diagram
     - Figma Design file has color variables, typography styles, and components
     - 5 screens visible in Figma with consistent design language
     - HTML prototype runs locally and navigates between all screens
     - All Figma files are shareable via link

> are you able to put those figma files into the B4G 2026 project i created     
within figma?                                                                   

● Unfortunately, the Figma MCP tools don't support moving files between projects
   — that's a file management operation outside the Plugin API scope.

  You can do it manually in a few clicks though:

  1. Go to your Figma file browser (figma.com/files)
  2. Find each file (they'll be in your Drafts or recent files):
    - Solace — Design System Starter
    - Solace — Astronaut Mental Wellness Prototype
  3. Right-click each file → Move to Project → select B4G 2026

  For the 3 FigJam diagrams, make sure you've claimed them first by opening each
   link, then move them the same way.

  Alternatively, you can open any file and click the file name in the top 
  toolbar → Move to project → B4G 2026.

● Skill(frontend-design:frontend-design)

● Good call — the current design is too cookie-cutter. Let me completely rethink
   the aesthetic.

  Design Direction: "Deep Space Observatory" — the quiet precision of a mission
  control interface meets the calm atmosphere of a meditation retreat. Think
  Interstellar UI meets Headspace, but with real craft.

  Key changes:
  - Fonts: Outfit (display) + DM Sans (body) + JetBrains Mono (data) — no more
  generic Inter
  - Atmosphere: Subtle starfield, radial gradients, glass-morphism cards with
  backdrop blur
  - Icons: Custom visual mood indicators instead of emoji
  - Layout: Asymmetric, generous negative space, visual hierarchy through scale
  - Color: Dominant near-black with a single striking teal glow accent

● The prototype has been completely redesigned. Here's what changed:

  Typography: Outfit (display headings) + DM Sans (body) + JetBrains Mono
  (data/labels) — no more generic Inter

  Atmosphere: Subtle radial gradient glows, noise texture overlay,
  glass-morphism cards with backdrop blur — real depth instead of flat cards

  Color refinement: Near-black void base (#03060B), single dominant teal glow
  with warm/lilac/sage as contextual accents — not evenly distributed

  Mood indicators: Custom gradient orbs with physics-based hover scaling instead
   of emoji

  Visual craft:
  - Gradient ring on wellness score (teal → lilac)
  - Crew mood shown as small gradient orbs, not emoji
  - Schedule items with subtle hover states
  - Message bubbles with proper glass-morphism
  - Earth delay shown as a gradient line separator
  - Navigation has a thin glow bar indicator instead of a dot

  Details: Monospaced labels for data/timestamps, uppercase tracking for section
   labels, lighter font weights for elegance, consistent 20px border radius on
  glass cards

  To preview it, restart the server:
  cd prototype && python -m http.server 3000
  Then open http://localhost:3000.

✻ Sautéed for 5m 16s
> still comes off as very vibe coded. make it nasa themed (dont go too          
overboard with it). get rid of dark mode.                                       

● Good feedback. The dark glassmorphism is peak AI aesthetic. Let me go light,
  clean, and NASA-informed — think mission control dashboards meets a
  well-designed institutional health app. White backgrounds, structured grids,
  NASA blue, no glow effects.

  - Light mode: White cards on light gray (#F7F8FA) background
  - NASA blue (#0B3D91) as the single primary color, warm orange for
  warnings/alerts, green for success

  blue=primary actions

✻ Worked for 4m 35s

> do you think its bad to stretch yourself too thin? like the design            
incorporates 3 of the 5 problem areas.                                          

● Honestly, for a competition submission, covering 3 areas well is stronger than
   covering 1 deeply — it shows systems thinking and demonstrates that you
  understand the interconnected nature of astronaut mental health (which is the
  actual NASA research reality — sleep affects stress, stress affects crew
  cohesion, etc.).

  The 5 screens handle it naturally without feeling thin:

  ┌────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │     Screen     │               Problem Area                │
  ├────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Home Dashboard │ Overview that ties everything together    │
  ├────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Check-In       │ Behavioral Health Monitoring              │
  ├────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Restore        │ Mindfulness & Mental Restoration          │
  ├────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Sleep Center   │ Sleep & Circadian Support                 │
  ├────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Crew Hub       │ Bonus: touches Connection & Communication │
  └────────────────┴───────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Each screen is focused and complete — none of them feel like a half-baked
  version of something bigger. The dashboard is what glues them together into a
  coherent product rather than 3 separate tools.

  If you wanted to narrow down, you'd cut Sleep or Crew and go deeper on the
  remaining ones. But I think the breadth is actually a strength here — it shows
   you designed a real product, not just a feature demo.

  Keep it as-is. The judges will see a cohesive app, not a scattered one.

> where does tribe v2 come in?                                                  

● TRIBE v2 is your research justification layer — it's the "why" behind your
  design decisions, not a feature in the app itself. The challenge brief says to
   show your research and problem framing, and TRIBE v2 gives you neuroscience
  backing instead of just "we thought this looked nice."

  Here's how it actually connects:

  ┌────────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │        Design Decision         │         TRIBE v2 Justification         │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Icon-only tab bar, no labels   │ Language network gets taxed by text —  │
  │                                │ reduce reading load                    │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Minimal/no animation on data   │ Motion network gets taxed — keep       │
  │ screens                        │ cognitive-load screens static          │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Nature sounds + visuals paired │ Auditory + visual networks work        │
  │  together in Restore           │ together for restoration               │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Journaling and reflection      │ Default mode network activates during  │
  │ features                       │ rest/introspection                     │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Clean, high-contrast layout    │ Visual network is sensitive to clutter │
  └────────────────────────────────┴────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Where it shows up in your submission:
  - FigJam brainstorm — the TRIBE v2 → Design System diagram maps this
  explicitly
  - Video walkthrough — you explain these connections verbally (the script
  already covers this)
  - Design system file — the "Design Principles (TRIBE v2)" section documents it

  Where it does NOT show up: anywhere in the actual app UI. It's not a feature —
   it's cited like you'd cite a research paper. "Our design decisions are
  informed by TRIBE v2's mapping of brain functional networks."

  That's exactly what the challenge wants — show that your design isn't
  arbitrary, it's grounded in real research. The FigJam board is where the
  judges will see that connection most clearly.

● OK, I've read the paper. Here's the honest truth:

  What the paper actually demonstrates:
  - TRIBE v2 predicts which brain regions activate for visual, audio, and text
  stimuli
  - Audio → auditory cortex, Video → occipital/parietal, Text → language cortex
  + prefrontal lobe
  - Multimodal stimuli (audio + visual combined) activate more brain area than
  either alone — biggest gains at the temporal-parietal-occipital junction
  - It replicates known neuroscience: FFA for faces, VWFA for written
  characters, Broca's area for syntax, TPJ for emotional processing

  What the paper does NOT say:
  - "Don't use text labels on navigation" — fabricated
  - "Animation taxes the motion network" — not in this paper
  - "Icon-forward design reduces cognitive load" — nowhere
  - Any UX design guidelines whatsoever

  I was extrapolating. Most of the "TRIBE v2 design principles" I created were
  me making plausible-sounding connections that aren't actually in the research.

  What you can legitimately cite:

  1. Multimodal restoration is backed — the paper directly shows audio+visual
  stimuli activate more of the brain than either alone (Figure 7C). This
  genuinely supports pairing nature sounds with nature visuals in the Restore
  hub.
  2. The demo at https://aidemos.atmeta.com/tribev2/ — you could potentially
  input different stimuli and screenshot the brain activation visualizations for
   your FigJam board. That would be real evidence, not hand-waving.
  3. The broader framing — TRIBE v2 is the kind of model that could someday
  power behavioral health monitoring (predicting stress from brain activity
  patterns). That's a legitimate future-looking connection for a concept app.

  My recommendation: Scale back the TRIBE v2 claims significantly. Instead of
  "every design decision is grounded in TRIBE v2," frame it as:
  - "TRIBE v2 research demonstrates that multimodal stimuli engage more brain
  regions, which informed our decision to pair audio + visual in restoration
  features"
  - "A model like TRIBE v2 represents the kind of neuroscience AI that could
  power real-time behavioral health monitoring in future space missions"
  - Use the demo to generate actual screenshots of brain activation for your
  FigJam board

  Do you want me to update the FigJam diagrams and video script to reflect
  honest, verifiable claims?

  Sources:
  - https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.22229
  - https://aidemos.atmeta.com/tribev2/
  - https://ai.meta.com/blog/tribe-v2-brain-predictive-foundation-model/
  - https://github.com/facebookresearch/tribev2

● Here's what I found:

  The web demo is at https://aidemos.atmeta.com/tribev2/ — it's a JavaScript
  app, so you need to visit it in your browser. I couldn't scrape it, but based
  on the descriptions it lets you explore pre-computed brain activation
  visualizations for different stimuli types.

  The real tool for comparing designs is the Colab notebook — this is where you
  could actually input your own stimuli and see predicted brain activation.
  Here's how it works:

  1. Go to the https://github.com/facebookresearch/tribev2 and click the Colab
  badge
  2. You can feed it:
    - An image/video (e.g., a screenshot of your UI with text-heavy layout vs.
  icon-heavy layout)
    - Audio (e.g., nature sounds)
    - Text (e.g., the text content in your app)
  3. It outputs a brain surface heatmap (~20k vertices) showing predicted fMRI
  activation

  What you could actually do for the submission:

  Take two screenshots — say, a text-heavy screen and an icon-heavy screen —
  feed them both through the Colab notebook, and compare the brain activation
  maps. If text-heavy inputs light up more language cortex / prefrontal regions
  (which the paper says they should — page 9, Figure 7B), that's real evidence
  you generated yourself.

  That would be genuinely impressive for a competition — not "I read about TRIBE
   v2," but "I used TRIBE v2 to compare design alternatives and the results
  informed my decisions."

  Practical steps:
  1. Open the Colab notebook from the
  https://github.com/facebookresearch/tribev2
  2. Screenshot 2-3 variations of your UI (text-heavy vs. minimal, static vs.
  animated)
  3. Run them through the model
  4. Screenshot the brain activation outputs
  5. Put those side-by-side in your FigJam brainstorm board
  6. That becomes your actual evidence

  Want me to update the FigJam diagrams and video script now to reflect only
  what's actually verifiable? I'd reframe around:
  - The multimodal finding (paper Figure 7 — audio + visual > either alone)
  - The future-use case for behavioral health monitoring
  - Your own Colab experiment results (once you run them)

  Sources:
  - https://github.com/facebookresearch/tribev2
  - https://aidemos.atmeta.com/tribev2/
  - https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.22229

● Created compare_designs.ipynb. Here's what it does:

  1. Install + load model — 2 cells, no fluff
  2. image_to_video() — converts a static screenshot to a 5-second video (TRIBE
  v2 needs video input)
  3. predict_for_image() — runs the model and returns averaged brain activation
  4. Upload — Colab file upload widget for your screenshots
  5. Side-by-side comparison — shows input images, then brain activation map for
   each
  6. Difference map — if you upload exactly 2 images, it subtracts them and
  shows which screen activates which regions more (red vs. blue)

  How to use it:

  1. Open it in Colab (upload the .ipynb or open from GitHub)
  2. Enable GPU runtime
  3. Run the install cell, restart runtime
  4. Run the rest
  5. Upload 2 screenshots — e.g., your Restore Hub (multimodal: sounds +
  visuals) vs. a text-heavy alternative
  6. Screenshot the brain maps and difference map for your FigJam board

  The difference map is the money shot — it'll show, in red and blue, which
  brain regions each design activates more. That's real evidence you generated
  yourself.

  plot_brain. The fix reshapes the averaged activation from (n_vertices,) to (1,

● This is great — you got real results. Here's what the brain plots show:

  Individual activation maps: Both designs light up visual cortex heavily
  (occipital region at the back — makes sense, they're both images). But look at
   the activation patterns — they're noticeably different in intensity and
  distribution.

  The difference map is the key finding:
  - Red/warm areas (back of the brain, occipital region) = more activation from
  the multimodal design — the visual imagery (trees, waves, mountains) is
  driving more visual cortex engagement
  - Blue/cool areas (spread across more of the cortex) = more activation from
  the text-heavy design — the dense text is recruiting more widespread
  processing, likely language areas

  This actually aligns with what the paper says (Figure 7B): visual stimuli
  dominate occipital/parietal cortex, text dominates language cortex and
  prefrontal lobe.

  What you can now say in your submission (honestly):

  ▎ "We ran both design alternatives through Meta FAIR's TRIBE v2 brain encoder.
   The multimodal design with nature imagery produced stronger activation in 
  visual processing regions, while the text-heavy design recruited more 
  widespread cortical processing. This supports our decision to use visual 
  imagery paired with audio cues in the Restore Hub — it engages targeted 
  sensory networks rather than demanding broad cognitive processing from an 
  already-fatigued astronaut."

  Put these screenshots on your FigJam board — that's real, self-generated
  evidence from a published model. No other team in this competition is going to
   have that.

✻ Worked for 31s

> can we utilize the model more other than just for design choices?             

● Yes — you're currently only using it to compare static screenshots. But the
  model takes video, audio, and text. Here are two more experiments you could
  run that directly tie to your app's features:

  1. Test the actual nature soundscapes (audio)

  Feed different nature audio clips through the model — forest rain, ocean
  waves, etc. Show that they activate auditory/temporal cortex in distinct
  patterns. This validates which restoration content to include in the app, not
  just how the UI looks.

  You can find free nature audio clips on sites like freesound.org, or generate
  them with TTS isn't needed — just find ~10 second .wav clips of rain, waves,
  birdsong.

  2. Multimodal vs. unimodal — the paper's core finding, demonstrated yourself

  This is the strongest experiment. Take one nature scene and feed it three
  ways:
  - Video only (nature video, muted)
  - Audio only (nature sounds, no video)
  - Video + audio together

  The paper predicts that the combined version activates more brain area (Figure
   7C). If you replicate that finding yourself, you can say:

  ▎ "We replicated TRIBE v2's multimodal finding with our own restoration 
  content. Audio+visual nature stimuli activated significantly more cortical 
  area than either modality alone — directly justifying Solace's paired 
  soundscape design."

  That's a published finding you independently verified with the app's own
  content. That's research-grade work for a design competition.

  3. Conceptual framing for behavioral health monitoring

  This isn't an experiment you run — it's how you frame the connection in your
  video. The model predicts brain responses to stimuli. Flip that: if astronauts
   wore lightweight neuroimaging (which NASA is researching), a model like TRIBE
   v2 could detect when brain responses deviate from baseline — early stress
  detection. Solace's Check-In screen is the manual version of what this AI
  could automate.

  Practical next step: I can update the Colab notebook to include cells for
  experiments 1 and 2. Want me to add those?

        1 # TRIBE v2 Experiments for Solace — Astronaut Mental Wellness A
          pp
        2 
        3 Three experiments using Meta FAIR's TRIBE v2 brain encoder to v
          alidate design decisions:
        4 
        5 1. **UI Design Comparison** — Compare multimodal vs. text-heavy
           screen layouts
        6 2. **Nature Soundscape Testing** — Compare brain responses to d
          ifferent nature audio
        7 3. **Multimodal vs. Unimodal** — Replicate the paper's core fin
          ding with our own content
        8 
        9 **Setup:** Google Colab with GPU (Runtime > Change runtime type
           > T4 GPU)  ⎿  Updated cell :
         1 import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
         2 from PIL import Image
         3 
         4 def image_to_video(image_path, output_path, duration=5, fps=24
           ):
         5     """Convert a static image to a short video so TRIBE v2 can
            process it."""
         6     img = cv2.imread(str(image_path))
         7     h, w = img.shape[:2]
         8     writer = cv2.VideoWriter(str(output_path), cv2.VideoWriter
           _fourcc(*'mp4v'), fps, (w, h))
         9     for _ in range(duration * fps):
        10         writer.write(img)
        11     writer.release()
        12     return output_path
        13 
        14 
        15 def predict_for_media(label, video_path=None, audio_path=None,
            text_path=None):
        16     """Run TRIBE v2 on any combination of video/audio/text. Re
           turns averaged activation."""
        17     kwargs = {}
        18     if video_path: kwargs["video_path"] = video_path
        19     if audio_path: kwargs["audio_path"] = audio_path
        20     if text_path:  kwargs["text_path"] = text_path
        21     df = model.get_events_dataframe(**kwargs)
        22     preds, segments = model.predict(events=df)
        23     return preds.mean(axis=0), preds, segments
        24 
        25 
        26 def predict_for_image(image_path, label):
        27     """Convenience: convert image to video, then predict."""
        28     video_path = CACHE / f"{label}.mp4"
        29     image_to_video(image_path, video_path)
        30     return predict_for_media(label, video_path=video_path)
        31 
        32 
        33 def plot_and_save(activation, title, filename, cmap="fire", **
           kwargs):
        34     """Plot a single brain map and save it."""
        35     fig = plotter.plot_timesteps(
        36         activation[np.newaxis, :],
        37         cmap=cmap,
        38         norm_percentile=99,
        39         **kwargs,
        40     )
        41     fig.suptitle(title, fontsize=16, fontweight="bold", y=1.02
           )
        42     fig.savefig(CACHE / filename, dpi=150, bbox_inches="tight"
           )
        43     plt.show()
        44     return fig
        45 
        46 
        47 def plot_difference(avg_a, avg_b, label_a, label_b, filename):
        48     """Plot difference map between two activations."""
        49     diff = avg_a - avg_b
        50     print(f"Positive (warm) = more activation in {label_a}")
        51     print(f"Negative (cool) = more activation in {label_b}")
        52     return plot_and_save(diff, f"Difference: {label_a} vs {lab
           el_b}", filename, cmap="RdBu_r")

● The audio experiments hit a LLaMA 3.2 access gate — the model tries to use
  LLaMA for text encoding when it transcribes speech from audio, and you need
  HuggingFace authentication for that.

  You need to:
  1. Go to https://huggingface.co/meta-llama/Llama-3.2-3B and request access
  (accept the license)
  2. Create a token at https://huggingface.co/settings/tokens
  3. In Colab, go to the key icon (left sidebar) → Add a secret called HF_TOKEN
  with your token value, and toggle "Notebook access" on
  4. Restart the runtime

  Then I can re-run Experiments 2 and 3. The image-based Experiment 1 worked
  because it only used the video extractor (no LLaMA needed), but audio input
  triggers speech transcription which requires LLaMA access.

  Let me know when you've set up the HF token.
